The Four Types of Reasoning
Sanusi enumerates four types of reasoning with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of reasoning (istidlal) as classified by Imam al-Sanusi, along with an alternative reduction to three types.

Core Idea
Reasoning is classified by the relationship between cause and effect.

Imam al-Sanusi begins a new section on reasoning (istidlal), which is essential for theological proof. He first defines the term and then enumerates four types, each differing by the logical direction between cause and effect. Understanding this classification is foundational before applying it to the knowledge of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify each type
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the overall classification. Then read the translation to ensure understanding of the four types.
Notice that Sanusi gives an example for each type.
2
Understand the examples given
For each type, reflect on the example: fire and burning for cause-effect, boiling water and heat for two effects of one cause, and God's knowledge for correlates.
The example for the fourth type involves knowing God directly, which will be important later.
3
Note the alternative reduction to three types
Sanusi mentions that some scholars reduce the fourth type to the second, limiting reasoning to three types. Consider why this might be debated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية
في الاستدلال
اعلم أنَّ الاستدلال على أربعة أضرب :
الأول : الاستدلال بالسببِ على المُسبَّبِ ؛ كالاستدلال بمس النارِ مثلاً على احتراقِ الممسوس
الثاني : عكسه ؛ وهو الاستدلال بالمُسبَّب على السبب ؛ كالاستدلال باحتراقِ الشيء مثلاً على مس النارِ له ، ومنه الاستدلال بوجودِ الأثر على وجودِ المؤثر
الثالثُ : الاستدلالُ بأحدِ مُسبّبي سببٍ واحدٍ على المُسبَّبِ الآخرِ كالاستدلال بغليان الماء المركَبِ في آنية على النار مثلاً على حرارته ، فإنَّ غليانَهُ وحرارتَهُ مُسبَّبانِ عن سبب واحد ؛ وهو مجاورة النارِ
•
الرابع : الاستدلال بأحدِ المتلازمين على الآخر ؛ كالاستدلال بوجوب كونِهِ جلَّ وعلا عالماً على وجوب قيام العلم به ، ومنهم مَنْ ردَّ
(1)
هذا إلى القسم الثاني ؛ وهو وحصر الاستدلال في الثلاثةِ الأُوَلِ الاستدلال بالمسبب على السبب
فإذا عرفت هذا : فالذي يصلحُ مِنْ هذه الأنواع لمعرفتِه تعالى
-
النوع الثاني والرابع ، أمَّا الأوَّلُ - وهو الاستدلال بالسبب على المسبَّبِ - فمحال في حقهِ تعالى ؛ لوجوبِ وجودِهِ ، فيستحيلُ أنْ يكون له سببٌ ، وبعينِ هذا يبطل في حقه القسم الثالث
(۱) لكون المسبب يستلزم السبب . ) عكاري » ( ق ٦٦ ) .
The second introduction: On reasoning. Know that reasoning is of four types: The first: reasoning from the cause to the effect, like reasoning from touching fire, for example, to the burning of what is touched. The second: its converse, which is reasoning from the effect to the cause, like reasoning from the burning of a thing, for example, to the touching of fire to it, and from it is reasoning from the existence of the effect to the existence of the cause. The third: reasoning from one of the two effects of a single cause to the other effect, like reasoning from the boiling of water placed in a vessel over fire, for example, to its heat, for its boiling and heat are two effects from one cause, which is the adjacency of the fire. The fourth: reasoning from one of two correlates to the other, like reasoning from the necessity of His being, glory be to Him and exalted, knowing, to the necessity of knowledge subsisting in Him. And among them is one who reduced this to the second division, and limited reasoning to the first three [types], i.e., reasoning from the effect to the cause. So if you know this, then what is suitable from these types for knowledge of Him, exalted is He, are the second and fourth types. As for the first — which is reasoning from the cause to the effect — it is impossible in His case, exalted is He, because of the necessity of His existence; thus it is impossible that He has a cause. And by the same reasoning the third division is invalid in His case. (1) Because the effect entails the cause. 'Akkari (q 66).
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a methodological classification followed by a theological application. It begins a new section called 'The second introduction: On reasoning.' Sanusi states that reasoning (istidlal) is of four types. He lists them in order. First: reasoning from cause to effect, e.g., from touching fire to the burning. Second: its converse, reasoning from effect to cause, e.g., from burning to the touching of fire; this includes reasoning from the existence of an effect to the existence of its cause. Third: reasoning from one effect of a single cause to another effect of the same cause, e.g., from boiling water (over fire) to its heat, since both are caused by the fire's adjacency. Fourth: reasoning from one of two correlates to the other, e.g., from the necessity that God is knowing to the necessity that knowledge subsists in Him. Sanusi notes that some scholars reduce the fourth type to the second (because a correlate is like an effect of the same cause? The footnote explains that the effect entails the cause). Then Sanusi gives the verdict: for knowing God, only the second and fourth types are suitable. The first type (cause to effect) is impossible regarding God because God's existence is necessary, so He cannot have a cause. By the same token, the third type is also invalid because it relies on a common cause, which God does not have.

Key Terms
الاستدلال
السبب
المسبب
المتلازمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There are exactly three types of reasoning with no reduction proposed.
Correction: Sanusi enumerates four types but notes that some scholars reduce the fourth to the second, so the number is not universally fixed.

Checkpoint
Which type of reasoning uses one effect to infer another effect of the same cause?
A) First
B) Second
C) Third
D) Fourth

Key Takeaway
Sanusi presents four types of reasoning, with the fourth type (correlates) sometimes reduced to the second (effect to cause).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these four types of reasoning do you most naturally rely on when thinking about Allah? Is it a cause-effect reasoning (e.g., creation indicates a Creator) or something else? How might an overreliance on one type limit your understanding of His attributes?
?
Sanusi notes that some scholars reduce the fourth type to the second. Are you willing to accept that scholarly disagreement can refine your tools for reasoning? Does it make you uncomfortable, or does it encourage you to study deeper?
?
When you encounter a theological issue, do you approach it with these distinct reasoning methods in mind, or do you jump to conclusions? What would change if you deliberately applied a different type of reasoning to a question about divine predestination?

Valid Reasoning for Knowing God
Sanusi identifies valid types of reasoning for knowing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which types of reasoning are valid for knowing God and why the first and third are impossible.

Core Idea
Only reasoning from effect to cause and from correlates are valid for God.

After classifying the four types of reasoning, Sanusi now applies them to the knowledge of God. He explains that because God is necessary in His existence, reasoning from a cause to an effect (type 1) and from one effect to another (type 3) are impossible. Only the second and fourth types are suitable.

Guided Path
1
Identify which types are valid
Read the passage and note that Sanusi explicitly says only the second and fourth types are suitable for knowing God.
2
Understand why type 1 is impossible
Type 1 (cause to effect) requires a cause for God, but God's existence is necessary (wajib), so He cannot have a cause. Reflect on why this makes type 1 impossible.
This is a key point in Islamic theology: God is uncaused.
3
See why type 3 is also invalid
Type 3 involves a common cause; since God has no cause, reasoning from one effect to another (both caused by something else) is invalid for God. The footnote adds that the effect entails the cause, reinforcing the point.

المقدمة الثانية
في الاستدلال
اعلم أنَّ الاستدلال على أربعة أضرب :
الأول : الاستدلال بالسببِ على المُسبَّبِ ؛ كالاستدلال بمس النارِ مثلاً على احتراقِ الممسوس
الثاني : عكسه ؛ وهو الاستدلال بالمُسبَّب على السبب ؛ كالاستدلال باحتراقِ الشيء مثلاً على مس النارِ له ، ومنه الاستدلال بوجودِ الأثر على وجودِ المؤثر
الثالثُ : الاستدلالُ بأحدِ مُسبّبي سببٍ واحدٍ على المُسبَّبِ الآخرِ كالاستدلال بغليان الماء المركَبِ في آنية على النار مثلاً على حرارته ، فإنَّ غليانَهُ وحرارتَهُ مُسبَّبانِ عن سبب واحد ؛ وهو مجاورة النارِ
•
الرابع : الاستدلال بأحدِ المتلازمين على الآخر ؛ كالاستدلال بوجوب كونِهِ جلَّ وعلا عالماً على وجوب قيام العلم به ، ومنهم مَنْ ردَّ
(1)
هذا إلى القسم الثاني ؛ وهو وحصر الاستدلال في الثلاثةِ الأُوَلِ الاستدلال بالمسبب على السبب
فإذا عرفت هذا : فالذي يصلحُ مِنْ هذه الأنواع لمعرفتِه تعالى
-
النوع الثاني والرابع ، أمَّا الأوَّلُ - وهو الاستدلال بالسبب على المسبَّبِ - فمحال في حقهِ تعالى ؛ لوجوبِ وجودِهِ ، فيستحيلُ أنْ يكون له سببٌ ، وبعينِ هذا يبطل في حقه القسم الثالث
(۱) لكون المسبب يستلزم السبب . ) عكاري » ( ق ٦٦ ) .
The second introduction: On reasoning. Know that reasoning is of four types: The first: reasoning from the cause to the effect, like reasoning from touching fire, for example, to the burning of what is touched. The second: its converse, which is reasoning from the effect to the cause, like reasoning from the burning of a thing, for example, to the touching of fire to it, and from it is reasoning from the existence of the effect to the existence of the cause. The third: reasoning from one of the two effects of a single cause to the other effect, like reasoning from the boiling of water placed in a vessel over fire, for example, to its heat, for its boiling and heat are two effects from one cause, which is the adjacency of the fire. The fourth: reasoning from one of two correlates to the other, like reasoning from the necessity of His being, glory be to Him and exalted, knowing, to the necessity of knowledge subsisting in Him. And among them is one who reduced this to the second division, and limited reasoning to the first three [types], i.e., reasoning from the effect to the cause. So if you know this, then what is suitable from these types for knowledge of Him, exalted is He, are the second and fourth types. As for the first — which is reasoning from the cause to the effect — it is impossible in His case, exalted is He, because of the necessity of His existence; thus it is impossible that He has a cause. And by the same reasoning the third division is invalid in His case. (1) Because the effect entails the cause. 'Akkari (q 66).
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a methodological classification followed by a theological application. It begins a new section called 'The second introduction: On reasoning.' Sanusi states that reasoning (istidlal) is of four types. He lists them in order. First: reasoning from cause to effect, e.g., from touching fire to the burning. Second: its converse, reasoning from effect to cause, e.g., from burning to the touching of fire; this includes reasoning from the existence of an effect to the existence of its cause. Third: reasoning from one effect of a single cause to another effect of the same cause, e.g., from boiling water (over fire) to its heat, since both are caused by the fire's adjacency. Fourth: reasoning from one of two correlates to the other, e.g., from the necessity that God is knowing to the necessity that knowledge subsists in Him. Sanusi notes that some scholars reduce the fourth type to the second (because a correlate is like an effect of the same cause? The footnote explains that the effect entails the cause). Then Sanusi gives the verdict: for knowing God, only the second and fourth types are suitable. The first type (cause to effect) is impossible regarding God because God's existence is necessary, so He cannot have a cause. By the same token, the third type is also invalid because it relies on a common cause, which God does not have.

Key Terms
الاستدلال
السبب
المسبب
وجوب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reasoning from cause to effect is a valid way to prove God's attributes.
Correction: Sanusi states that type 1 (cause to effect) is impossible for God because God has no cause; only reasoning from effect to cause (type 2) is valid.

Checkpoint
Why is reasoning from cause to effect (type 1) impossible for God according to Sanusi?
A) God is not a cause
B) God's existence is necessary, so He cannot have a cause
C) God is too great to be known by reasoning
D) It is only invalid in some cases

Key Takeaway
For knowing God, only reasoning from effects to a cause (type 2) and from correlates (type 4) are valid; types 1 and 3 are impossible because God is necessary and uncaused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is necessary and uncaused, what does that mean for your own sense of self-sufficiency? Are there moments when you live as though you are the cause of your own success or failure? How can you shift your mindset to see all effects as pointing to Him?
?
Sanusi says that reasoning from cause to effect is impossible for God because He has no cause. Have you ever thought of God as 'the First Cause'? How does this lesson refine that understanding? Does it change the way you think about God's relationship to creation?
?
The only valid reasoning to God is from effects to a cause. Reflect on one effect in your life—a blessing, a trial, or a sign—that you have not consciously linked to God. Will you begin to actively contemplate such signs as proofs of His existence starting today?

The Method of Mixing Huduth and Imkan
Sanusi identifies the combined huduth-imkan method and attributes it to Imam al-Haramayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means to combine the arguments from huduth (origination) and imkan (contingency) in proving the existence of the Creator, and why Imam al-Haramayn relied on this method.

Core Idea
The strongest proof for God's existence combines two distinct rational arguments into one.

In this passage, Sanusi (via Ibn al-Tilmisani) identifies a specific theological methodology: mixing the proof from origination (huduth) with the proof from contingency (imkan). This combined approach was favored by the great Ash'ari theologian Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). Understanding this method helps us see how classical scholars integrated multiple rational proofs to strengthen their case for the Creator's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the phrase 'yashubu al-hudutha bi al-imkan'.
2
Identify the two components
Identify huduth (origination) and imkan (contingency). Reflect on why combining them might be stronger than using each alone.
Huduth argues from temporal beginning; imkan from essential dependence.
3
Recognize the authority
Note that Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) relied on this method. Consider why his endorsement is significant in Ash'ari theology.
Al-Juwayni is a major figure in classical kalam.
4
Reflect on the phrase 'mixing'
Consider what 'yashubu' implies about the relationship between huduth and imkan in the argument. Are they separate proofs or intertwined?
The Arabic verb suggests blending, not just juxtaposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال معناه شرف الدين بن التلمساني (١) وهذه الطريقةُ - أعني : طريقةُ مَنْ يستدل على افتقار الحادثِ إلى الصانع - طريقةُ مَنْ يشوبُ الحدوثَ بالإمكان عند الاستدلال على وجودِ الصانع ، وعلى هذه الطريقةِ عوَّلَ إمام الحرمين
Sharaf al‑Din ibn al‑Tilmisani said its meaning: And this method—I mean the method of one who argues for the contingent's need for the Maker—is the method of one who mixes origination with contingency when arguing for the existence of the Maker, and upon this method Imam al‑Haramayn relied.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This short passage is a statement about the method used in proving the existence of the Creator. Sanusi, quoting or commenting through Ibn al‑Tilmisani, characterizes the method as one that 'mixes' (yashubu) origination (huduth) with contingency (imkan). This is the approach of those who combine these two attributes in their argument for the need of a Creator. He notes that this very method was relied upon by Imam al‑Haramayn (probably al‑Juwayni). The passage thus identifies a specific theological methodology and its prominent advocate.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof from huduth and the proof from imkan are always used separately and independently.
Correction: Classical theologians sometimes combined them into a single, more powerful argument, as did Imam al‑Haramayn.

Checkpoint

B) Imam al‑Haramayn
C) Al‑Baydawi

Key Takeaway
The method of mixing huduth and imkan was the preferred approach of Imam al‑Haramayn for proving the existence of the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly internalized that every aspect of your existence—your breath, your heartbeat, your thoughts—is contingent on the constant act of the Maker, how would your prayer change tonight?
?
What would it mean for you to 'mix' the intellectual proof of God's existence with the tangible evidence of your own dependence on Him? Are you currently relying on one more than the other?
?
This method reveals that you are not self-sufficient. When was the last time you felt that dependence so deeply that it moved you to tears or prostration? If never, what is holding you back from that reality?

The 'I Was Not Then I Was' Argument and the Distinctive Method
Sanusi explains the distinctive method and unpacks the 'I was not then I was' argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinctive method of the mutakallimun in proving the Creator through the concise 'I was not then I was' argument, and how it differs from other approaches.

Core Idea
The simplest proof: I existed after non-existence, so I have a Creator.

Sanusi now clarifies what makes the kalam method unique compared to other theological proofs. He unpacks a very concise version of the argument from contingent existence, showing that the major premise is so self-evident it can be omitted. Then he defends the argument against a potential objection. This short passage packs in a complete logical demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow.
Notice how Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the minor and major premises
The argument is given with only the minor premise 'I was not then I was'. The major premise 'Everyone who was not then was has a Creator' is omitted. Find where Sanusi mentions this.
3
Understand why the major premise is omitted
Sanusi says it is omitted 'because it is known'. Reflect on why this might be self-evident to the audience.
This is a key point about the method: relying on necessary knowledge.
4
Follow the objection and its refutation
Sanusi presents an objection: if you could create yourself, you could create what is easier. The refutation uses the equality of possibles. Trace the logical steps.
5
Grasp the entailment about ability
The final sentence explains that ability to invent one possible implies ability to invent another like it, because possibles are equal in possibility. This is the core of the objection's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأنت ترى كيف تأخَّرَ العلم بحدوثِ العالم في هذه الطريقة عنِ العلم بوجودِ الصانع ، فقد ظهر الفرقُ بينَ هذه الطريقةِ وغيرِها مِنَ الطرق .
قوله : ( فتعلم أنَّ لكَ مُوجِداً أوجدَكَ ) يعني : غيرَكَ ؛ بدليل ما بعد ، وهذا نتيجة الدليل المذكور ، إلا أنَّه استغنى فيهِ بذكرِ المقدمة الصغرى ؛ وهي قولنا : ( أنا لم أكن ثم كنتُ ) ، وحذفَ الكبرى ؛ وهي قولنا : ( وكلُّ مَنْ لم يكن ثم كانَ فلهُ مُوجِدٌ أوجده ) . . للعلم بها قوله : ( لاستحالةِ أنْ تُوجِدَ نفسَكَ ) يعني : أَنَّكَ لمَّا احتجت إلى مُرجح لوجودِكَ على عدمِكَ السابق .. لزمَ أنْ يكونَ ذلكَ المرجّحُ غيركَ .
قوله : ( وإلا لأمكن أن تُوجِدَ ما هو أهون عليكَ مِنْ نفسِكَ ( تقريره أن تقول : لو أمكن أن تُوجِدَ نفسَكَ .. لأمكن أن تُوجِدَ ذاتَ غيرِكَ ، والتالي باطل ، فالمقدَّم مثله
بيانُ الملازمة : أنَّ القدرة على اختراع أحد المثلين قدرة على اختراع مِثْلِهِ ، والممكنات متساوية في الإمكان المصحح لتعلقِ القدرة ، فالقدرة على إيجادِ بعضها اختراعاً قدرة على إيجاد جميعها
So you see how knowledge of the creation of the world in this method comes after knowledge of the existence of the Maker. Thus the difference between this method and other methods has become clear.
His words: (So you know that you have a Creator who created you) means: other than you, as evidenced by what follows, and this is the result of the mentioned proof, except that in it he sufficed by mentioning the minor premise, which is our saying: (I was not then I was), and he omitted the major premise, which is our saying: (And everyone who was not then was has a Creator who created him) ... because it is known. His words: (Due to the impossibility that you create yourself) means: since you needed a preponderator for your existence over your prior non-existence, it necessarily follows that that preponderator is other than you.
His words: (Otherwise it would be possible for you to create what is easier for you than yourself). Its exposition is that you say: If it were possible for you to create yourself, then it would be possible for you to create the essence of another. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. The explanation of the entailment: that the ability to invent one of two likes is an ability to invent its like, and possibles are equal in the possibility that enables the attachment of ability. So the ability to bring into existence some of them by invention is an ability to bring into existence all of them.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the distinctive method of the mutakallimun and an elaboration of the 'I was not then I was' argument for the existence of a Creator. Sanusi first points out that in this method, knowledge of the world's creation follows knowledge of the Maker's existence, unlike other methods. This shows the difference.
Then he explains a specific phrase from the matn: 'So you know that you have a Creator who created you.' This is the conclusion of the proof. The proof is given concisely by stating only the minor premise: 'I was not then I was.' The major premise – 'Everyone who was not then was has a Creator who created him' – is omitted because it is self-evident. The reason for this is that if you try to create yourself, you would need a preponderator (مرجح) to tip the scales from non-existence to existence, and that preponderator cannot be yourself, so it must be another.
Sanusi then discusses an objection: 'Otherwise it would be possible for you to create what is easier for you than yourself.' The exposition (تقرير) is that if you could create yourself, you could create another like yourself. The consequent is false because obviously we cannot create others. Therefore, the antecedent (the ability to create yourself) is also false. The entailment (ملازمة) is explained: the power to invent one of two likes is the power to invent its like. Since all possibles are equal in the possibility (إمكان) that allows power to attach to them, if you could create any one of them, you could create all of them. Since you cannot create all, you cannot create even yourself.

Key Terms
مقدمة صغرى
كبرى
مرجح
قدرة
اختراع
مِثْل
إمكان
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the 'I was not then I was' argument is incomplete or relies on an unstated assumption that is not obvious.
Correction: Sanusi explains that the major premise ('Everyone who was not then was has a Creator') is so self-evident that it can be omitted without weakening the argument. The need for a preponderator external to oneself is logically necessary.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi say the major premise ('Everyone who was not then was has a Creator') can be omitted?
A) Because it is false
B) Because it is known (معلوم) without explicit mention
C) Because it is the same as the minor premise
D) Because it is not necessary for the argument

Key Takeaway
The 'I was not then I was' argument works because the need for a preponderator external to oneself is self-evident, and the objection claiming otherwise is refuted by the equality of possibles in possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that your own existence—the fact that you were not then you were—is a direct proof that you have a Creator. Have you ever truly stopped to let that truth sink into your bones? If you really believed that every breath you take is a gift from the One who created you from nothing, would you still live as if you own your own life?
?
Consider the preponderator: something outside of you tipped the scales for you to exist rather than remain non-existent. That preponderator is your Creator. In what area of your life do you most act as if you are the one who tips the scales, deciding your own path without reference to Him? What would it mean to actually hand the scales over to Him today?
?
The argument shows that if you could create yourself, you could create others—but you cannot. So you are entirely dependent. Does this truth make you feel small and helpless, or does it fill you with awe and gratitude? Be honest: when you look at your own abilities and achievements, do you secretly feel you are self-made? Ask yourself right now: What am I truly relying on for my existence and success?

Grammatical Analysis of the Proof's Premise
Sanusi parses the sentence 'wa an yastahilu' and explains key terms of the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi's grammatical parsing clarifies the logical structure of the proof, including the meaning of 'tatbiq' and the roles of 'mutabbaq' and 'mutabbaq 'alayhi'.

Core Idea
Grammatical precision reveals the logical backbone of the proof.

Sanusi now steps into a detailed grammatical analysis of his own proof. He breaks down the sentence word by word, showing how each part connects, and defines the technical terms 'tatbiq', 'mutabbaq', and 'mutabbaq 'alayhi'. This careful parsing ensures that the logical steps of the proof are unmistakable.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parenthetical case endings in the Arabic.
2
Identify the grammatical components
Follow Sanusi's parsing: find the coordinate phrase 'wa an yastahila', the subject of 'yalzamu', the referent of 'minha', and the circumstantial accusative 'bila ziyada'.
This step reinforces Arabic grammar in theological context.
3
Understand 'tatbiq' and the distinction between 'mutabbaq' and 'mutabbaq 'alayhi'
Sanusi defines 'tatbiq' as placing one thing upon another, i.e., comparing two numbers. Then he applies this to the example: the 'mutabbaq' is the number from the Flood to eternity, and the 'mutabbaq 'alayhi' is that same number with the addition of events from the Flood to now.
They are not two distinct sets but the same set considered before and after addition.
4
Appreciate why the proof is named 'cutting and application'
The proof is called 'cutting and application' because we temporarily 'cut off' the addition of events to compare the set with itself after the addition.
This name encapsulates the logical maneuver.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقولي : ( و أنْ يستحيل ( معطوفٌ على ( أنْ يقارنَ ) الذي هو فاعلُ ( يلزم ) ، والضمير المجرورُ في ( منها ( يعودُ على الحوادثِ ، و ( بدون زيادة ) حالٌ مِنْ فاعل ( فَرَغَ ) ، وقوله : ( على نفسِهِ ) يتعلَّقُ بـ ( تطبيق ) ، والتطبيق : جعلُ شيءٍ على شيء ، والمراد هنا : نظر أحد العددين مع الآخرِ ، و ( ما ) الموصولة في قولي : ( ما علم ) فاعلُ ( يستحيل ) والمطبّقُ مِنَ الحوادثِ نظيره في مثالنا : ما فرضناهُ مِنْ عددِ الحوادثِ مِنْ زمن الطوفانِ إلى الأزلِ ، والمطبق عليه : ما فرضناهُ مِنْ عددِ الحوادثِ مِنَ الآنَ إلى الأزلِ ، وهو في الحقيقة عين المطبق لكن بعد زيادة حوادث عليهِ ؛ وهو ما مِنَ الطوفانِ إلى الآنَ ، ولأجل قطعِنا في هذا البرهان المطبقِ عن زيادة حوادث لننظرَهُ معَ نفسِهِ بعد زيادتها .. سُمّي برهان القطع والتطبيق .
So my statement: 'and that it is impossible' is a co-ordinate to 'that it accompanies' which is the subject of 'it necessarily entails', and the genitive pronoun in 'from them' refers back to the events, and 'without increase' is a circumstantial accusative from the subject of 'emptied', and his statement: 'to itself' is attached to 'applying', and 'applying' (tatbiq) is: placing one thing upon another, and its meaning here is: considering one of the two numbers along with the other. And the relative pronoun 'what' in my statement 'what is known' is the subject of 'is impossible'. And the applied (mutabbaq) from the events is its counterpart in our example: what we supposed of the number of events from the time of the Flood to eternity, and the applied-upon (mutabbaq 'alayhi) is what we supposed of the number of events from now to eternity, and it is in reality the same as the applied but after an addition of events upon it; and it is what is from the Flood until now. And because we cut off in this proof the applied from the addition of events in order to consider it with itself after its addition, it is called the proof of cutting and application.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and terminological commentary on the earlier proof sentence 'wa an yastahila …'. Sanusi breaks down the syntax: 'wa an yastahila' (and that it is impossible) is conjoined to 'an yuqarina' (that it accompanies), which is the subject of 'yalzamu' (it necessarily entails). The pronoun 'minha' (from them) refers to the temporal events ('hawadith'), and 'bila ziyada' (without increase) is a circumstantial accusative describing the state of the subject of 'faragha' (it emptied). The phrase 'ala nafsihi' (to itself) is attached to 'tatbiq' (application), defined as placing one thing upon another, i.e., comparing two numbers. He then identifies the subject of 'yastahilu' (is impossible) as the relative clause 'ma 'ulima' (what is known). Next, he clarifies the terms 'mutabbaq' (the applied) and 'mutabbaq 'alayhi' (the applied-upon) using the Flood-to-eternity and now-to-eternity examples: the applied is the number from the Flood to eternity (the baseline), and the applied-upon is the number from now to eternity (which is actually the same set but with the addition of events from Flood to now). The reason the proof is called 'cutting and application' is that we 'cut off' that addition temporarily to compare it with itself after the addition. Thus the grammatical analysis reinforces the logical structure of the proof.

Key Terms
تطبيق
مطبق
مطبق عليه
زيادة
قطع
معطوف
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'mutabbaq' and 'mutabbaq 'alayhi' are two distinct infinite sets.
Correction: The commentary clarifies they are the same actual set considered before and after an addition.

Checkpoint
According to the grammatical commentary, what does the term 'tatbiq' mean in the context of this proof?
A) The act of cutting off events
B) The act of counting events
C) The act of placing one thing upon another, i.e., comparing two numbers
D) The act of proving the existence of God

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's grammatical analysis reveals that the proof of cutting and application relies on comparing the same set of events before and after an addition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the phrase 'bila ziyada' (without increase), what does it reveal about the need for precision in your own understanding of theological truth? Are you aware of any 'additions'—extra assumptions or unchecked ideas—that you may have unknowingly attached to your faith? Can you bring yourself to cut them off to see the truth more clearly?
?
Sanusi's grammatical breakdown shows that every word has its exact role. In your spiritual life, is every action, intention, and belief placed exactly where it belongs? What is one area where you might be misplacing something (e.g., giving worldly concerns the role of ultimate importance)?
?
The proof of cutting and application forces you to see that the same set of events, when considered with an addition, appears different. Is there a relationship or situation in your life where you have treated something as separate from your faith when it is actually part of it? What step can you take today to reintegrate it?

The Grave Danger of Neglecting Tawhid and a Prayer for Guidance
The commentator warns of the grave danger of neglecting tawhid and ends with a prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the severe consequence of neglecting the science of tawhid and the commentator's prayer for divine guidance.

Core Idea
Neglecting tawhid leads to eternal disgrace; only divine guidance saves.

In this brief but powerful passage, the commentator draws attention to the immense risk involved in ignoring the study of tawhid. He warns that such neglect equates to exposing oneself to eternal punishment alongside disbelievers. Following this solemn warning, he turns to Allah in a heartfelt prayer, seeking rectification of both outward and inward states and guidance to the straight path.

Guided Path
1
Read the warning
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the strong language used to describe the danger.
Pay attention to the words 'خطر عظيم' and 'العذاب المؤيَّد'.
2

Understand that turning away from tawhid leads to eternal punishment and disgrace in Hell.

3
Examine the prayer
Notice how the commentator transitions from warning to supplication, asking for rectification and guidance, mirroring the Fatiha.
The prayer includes asking for guidance to the path of those favored, avoiding the paths of wrath and error.

This prayer reflects the commentator's deep concern for correct belief and reliance on divine guidance.

Interpreting Divine Attributes and the Proof of Allah's Speech
Sanusi states the principle of literal interpretation for divine attributes and gives the scriptural proof for God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine attributes should be interpreted literally unless a contextual indicator requires metaphorical interpretation, and that the attribute of speech is proven by the consensus of prophets and Muslims.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are literal by default; metaphor needs evidence; consensus proves Allah's speech.

Sanusi establishes a key hermeneutical rule for understanding divine attributes: the apparent meaning (zahir) is the default, and deviation to metaphor (majaz) is only permissible when a contextual indicator (qarina) is present. He then applies this to the attribute of speech (kalam), citing the unanimous consensus of all prophets and the Muslim community as decisive scriptural proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi first presents a general principle then gives a specific application.
Pay attention to the conditional structure in the principle.
2
Identify the default rule for divine attributes
The passage states that we must believe what the verses apparently indicate. There is no need for interpretation (ta'wil) unless a proof prevents the apparent meaning. Find this statement.
The default is literal (zahir).
3
Understand the condition for metaphorical interpretation
Metaphor (majaz) requires a contextual indicator (qarina). Without it, resorting to metaphorical meaning is invalid. Identify why it is invalid (affirming the conditioned without its condition).
This guards against arbitrary interpretation.
4
Examine the proof for divine speech
Sanusi cites Imam al-Fakhr and Ibn al-Tilimsani to show that the prophets and the Muslim community unanimously agree that Allah is mutakallim (speaker). Note that the consensus is general, though details may differ.
Consensus (ijma') is a strong scriptural proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفات الجسمانية، ودل التصريح بهما على أنهما صفتا كمال. وجب اعتقاد ما دلت عليه الآي، ولا محوج للتأويل لا عقلاً ولا سمعاً، وحمل اللفظ على احتماله البعيد مجاز، وشرطه القرينة، ومع عدمها لا يجوز المصير إليه؛ لما فيه من إثبات المشروط بدون شرطه، فتعين البقاء مع تلك الظواهر، وهكذا القول ما ورد من أحكام الآخرة، متى كان ظاهره جائزاً وجب في جميع اعتقاده، إلا أن يدل دليل على امتناعه. وأما دليل كونه تعالى متكلماً من السمع: فقال الإمام الفخر: (أجمع الأنبياء والرسل على كونه تعالى متكلماً) ((1)) قال ابن التلمساني: (وقد أجمع المسلمون أيضاً على ذلك على الجملة، وإن اختلفوا في تفسير الكلام) ((2)).
The bodily attributes, and their explicit mention indicates they are attributes of perfection. It is obligatory to believe what the verses indicate, and there is no need for interpretation (ta'wil) neither by reason nor by scripture. Carrying the word to its remote possibility is metaphor (majaz), and its condition is a contextual indicator (qarina). Without it, it is not permissible to resort to it, because that would be affirming the conditioned without its condition. So remaining with those apparent meanings (zawahir) is determined. And likewise the sayings regarding the rulings of the afterlife, when its apparent meaning is possible, it is obligatory to believe in all of it, unless a proof indicates its impossibility. As for the proof that God is mutakallim (speaker) from scripture: Imam al-Fakhr said: 'The prophets and messengers have unanimously agreed that God is mutakallim.' Ibn al-Tilimsani said: 'The Muslims have also unanimously agreed on that in general, even if they differed in the interpretation of speech.'
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage first establishes a principle for interpreting divine attributes: the apparent meaning (zahir) is to be taken as is unless there is a proof that makes it impossible. Then it applies this to the attribute of speech (kalam). The proof from scripture is the consensus (ijma') of the prophets and messengers, and the consensus of the Muslim community. This shows that God's speech is an established attribute.

Key Terms

تأويل
مجاز
قرينة

سمع
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) of divine attributes is always permissible or even preferable.
Correction: The passage clarifies that metaphor requires a contextual indicator (qarina); without it, the apparent meaning must be retained. Arbitrary metaphorical interpretation is invalid.

Checkpoint

A) The apparent meaning must be problematic.
B) There must be a contextual indicator (qarina).
C) Reason alone permits it.

Key Takeaway
The apparent meaning of divine attributes is the default; metaphor requires evidence, and God's speech is proven by the consensus of prophets and Muslims.

Philosophers' Two Theories of Vision
Sanusi reports the philosophers' two theories on how vision occurs: by impression or by mediation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two theories of vision held by philosophers and the anatomical details they invoked.

Core Idea
Philosophers disagreed whether we perceive the image itself or the external object itself.

Before engaging with the Sunni position on Allah's vision, Sanusi first reports the philosophers' explanation of human vision. Their two competing theories set the stage for the theological discussion. This passage presents both views clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the philosophers define vision as an impression in the eye.
2
Identify the two opinions
The passage explicitly says 'they have two opinions'. Find the first opinion: what is perceived is the impressed form. Find the second: what is perceived is the external object via the impression.
3
Understand the anatomy involved
The second opinion mentions the crystalline humor and the common sense. Note their functions: the humor transmits the impression to the common sense in the brain.
4
Reflect on the implications
Why does this matter for theology? The philosophers' discussion of vision sets up the question of whether Allah can be seen. Sanusi will later argue from the Sunni perspective.
This is just a preview; the lesson focused on the passage itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبَتِ الفلاسفة إلى أنَّ معنى الرؤية تأثر الحدقة بسبب ارتسام صورة المبصر فيها ، ولهم قولان : أحدهما : أنَّ المدرَكَ لنا نفس المثال المنطبع ، وهو الشيء المطابق لما في الخارج ، الخالي عن المادة والثاني : أنَّ المدرَكَ لنا عين ذلكَ الشيء بواسطة المثال المنطبع في الرطوبة الجليديّةِ المؤدِّيةِ إلى الحس المشترك ؛ الذي هو مُركَّبٌ مِنْ عضلتين مجوّفتين على صورة صليب في مُقدَّم الدماغ
And the philosophers held that the meaning of vision is an affection of the pupil due to the impression of the image of the seen object in it. And they have two opinions: One: that what is perceived by us is the impressed form itself, and it is the thing corresponding to what is in the external [world], devoid of matter. And the second: that what is perceived by us is the very same external thing by means of the form impressed in the crystalline humor leading to the common sense; which is composed of two hollow muscles in the shape of a cross in the front of the brain.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' definition of vision (الرؤية) as the impression of the object's image in the eye. They then give two opinions: First, that what we perceive is the impressed image itself (المثال المنطبع), which corresponds to the external object but is immaterial. Second, that what we perceive is the external object itself, but mediated by the impressed image in the crystalline humor (الرطوبة الجليديّة), which then transmits to the common sense (الحس المشترك)—a faculty located in the front of the brain composed of two hollow muscles. The author reports these views without endorsing them, setting up the Sunni position later.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الفلاسفة
المثال المنطبع
الحس المشترك
الرطوبة الجليدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers all agree on how vision works.
Correction: In fact, they had two different theories: one says we perceive the image, the other says we perceive the object via the image.

Checkpoint

A) The impressed form only
B) The external object directly without mediation
C) The external object by means of the impressed form
D) The crystalline humor

Key Takeaway
The philosophers had two main theories of vision: direct perception of the impressed form, or perception of the external object mediated by the impressed form in the eye.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Philosophers' Explanation of Hearing
Sanusi explains the philosophers' mechanical account of hearing through sound waves and the common sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' mechanical explanation of hearing, involving sound waves, the ear canal, nerve, and the role of the common sense.

Core Idea
Hearing, for the philosophers, is a physical process of vibrations transmitted to a common sense.

Sanusi presents the philosophers' detailed account of hearing, describing how sound waves strike the ear, produce a ringing in a nerve, and are then perceived either directly by a faculty or via the 'common sense' — a kind of central processor for sensory data. This passage sets up a mechanistic view that the Sunni theologians will later challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the author's description of the hearing process sink in before analyzing it.
2
Identify the physical components
Notice the sequence: sound strikes stationary air in the ear canal, which is adjacent to a nerve stretched like skin on a drum. This produces a ringing.
The ear canal (الصماخ) and nerve are key anatomical terms.
3
Understand the two opinions on perception
There are two views: either the perceiving power in the nerve directly senses the ringing, or it transmits to the common sense. The common sense is likened to a basin receiving input from the five senses, and the soul perceives through it like reading a tablet.
Think of the common sense as a central processor that integrates sensory data.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : وأما السمعُ : فإنَّ الصوت وما يتركَّبُ مِنَ الحروفِ والأصواتِ إذا صادمَتِ الهواء الراكد في الصماخ المجاور للعصبة المفروشة في أقصى الصماخ ، الممدودة عليه كالجلدِ على الطبل . . حصل فيها طنين ، فتشعرُ بهِ القُوَّةُ المُدرِكَةُ المُودعةُ في تلكَ العصبة على رأي ، أو تؤدِّيهِ إلى الحس المشترك على رأي ، والحس المشترك على هذا الرأي كحوض تصب فيه خمسة أنابيب ؛ وهي الحواس الخمسُ ، ولذا سُمِّيَ مشتركاً ، والنفس هي المُدرِكةُ بواسطته كلوح تقروه
They said: As for hearing: sound and what is composed of letters and sounds—when they strike the stationary air in the ear canal adjacent to the nerve spread in the deepest part of the ear canal, extended over it like skin on a drum . . . a ringing occurs in it, then the perceiving power deposited in that nerve senses it according to one opinion, or it transmits it to the common sense according to another opinion. And the common sense according to this opinion is like a basin into which five pipes pour; and they are the five senses, and therefore it is called 'common'. And the soul perceives by means of it like a tablet being read.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage gives the philosophers' explanation of hearing. Sound, composed of letters and sounds, strikes the stagnant air in the ear canal (الصماخ). This air is adjacent to a nerve spread in the deepest part, stretched like skin on a drum. The impact produces a ringing (طنين). Then, according to one view, the perceiving power (القوة المدركة) in that nerve directly senses it; according to another, it transmits the sensation to the common sense (الحس المشترك). The common sense is likened to a basin receiving input from the five senses (the five pipes). The soul (النفس) then perceives through the common sense, like reading a tablet. This shows the philosophers' detailed mechanistic approach, which the Sunni school will later critique.

Key Terms
السمع
الصوت
الصماخ
القوة المدركة
الحس المشترك

Checkpoint

A) It transmits the sound to the soul directly
B) It senses the ringing itself
C) It sends the sound to the brain
D) It creates the sound

Key Takeaway
The philosophers explain hearing as a mechanical process culminating in perception by the soul through the common sense.

The Three Opinions on God as Perceiver
Sanusi investigates whether God is described as perceiver, presents three opinions, and prefers waqf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three opinions regarding God being 'perceiver' and why the author prefers suspending judgment.

Core Idea
When revelation is silent, we pause—we do not affirm or deny.

Sanusi now tackles a subtle attribute: is God described as 'perceiver' (mudrik)? Revelation explicitly mentions hearing, sight, and speech, but not perception. This silence forces a methodological choice. In this lesson, we explore the three scholarly positions and see why Sanusi opts for suspension (waqf).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without translating. Let the technical terms stand out: تحقيق, مدرك, وقف, سمع.
Notice the structure: investigation → position → reason → three opinions → preference.
2
Identify the three opinions
The passage mentions three opinions: affirmation (by those who see it as proven rationally), negation (due to entailment of connection to bodies), and suspension. List them in your notes.
Suspension is presented as the closest to the truth.
3
Understand the key reason for waqf
The author argues that in matters of negating deficiencies in God, we rely on revelation. Since revelation mentions hearing, sight, and speech but not perception, we cannot affirm or deny it categorically.
This is a methodological principle: the absence of scriptural mention leads to suspension.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some might think 'perceiver' is the same as 'knowing'. The passage hints that perception might be distinct and that it doesn't entail connection to bodies, contrary to what some scholars thought.
This protects against overextending attributes beyond revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحقيق كونه تعالى مُدركاً وبهذا يثبتُ كونُهُ مُدْرِكاً عندَ مَنْ أثبته ، والتحقيق فيه : الوقفُ ؛ لما تقدَّمَ أنَّ التحقيق في نفي النقائص الاعتماد على السمع ، وقد ورد في السمع والبصر والكلام ، ولم يرد في الإدراك ، وجزم بعضُهم بنفيهِ لمَّا رآه ملزوماً للاتصال بالأجسام؛ يعني : ويدخل في العلم والحقُّ: أنَّه لا يستلزمه. وبالجملة : فمجموع ما فيه ثلاثة أقوال ، وأقربها الوقف كما قدمناه
Investigation of His being, exalted, 'perceiver'. And by this, His being 'perceiver' is established for those who affirm it. But the verified position regarding it is suspension (al-waqf), because it has preceded that the verification in negating deficiencies relies on revelation, and revelation has come concerning hearing, sight, and speech, but has not come concerning perception. And some of them decisively affirmed its negation because they saw it as entailing connection to bodies—meaning it enters into knowledge, but the truth is that it does not entail it. In summary: the total of what is in it is three opinions, and the closest of them is suspension as we have presented.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents the investigation (tahqīq) of whether God is described as 'perceiver' (mudrik). The author notes that for those who affirm it, it is proven by a rational argument, but the verified position (al-tahqīq) is suspension (al-waqf). The reason is that the principle for negating deficiencies in God is to rely on revelation (al-sam'), and revelation has explicitly mentioned hearing (sam'), sight (baṣar), and speech (kalām), but it has not mentioned perception (idrāk). Some scholars decisively negated perception because they thought it necessarily entails connection to bodies, but in fact it does not entail that. So there are three opinions: affirmation, negation, and suspension. The author concludes that the soundest view is suspension, as he has previously stated.

Key Terms
مدرك
إدراك
وقف
سمع
تحقيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'perceiver' is a necessary attribute of God or that it is equivalent to knowing.
Correction: The passage clarifies that perception is not scripturally warranted, and even if it entails knowledge, it is not the same as the attribute of knowledge. Moreover, the entailment to connection with bodies is false.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the verified position regarding whether God is 'perceiver' is suspension (waqf)?
A) Because there is a consensus that God is not a perceiver.
B) Because revelation only mentions hearing, sight, and speech, not perception, so we cannot affirm or negate it definitively.
C) Because reason cannot prove either affirmation or negation.
D) Because the Mu'tazilites forced the issue.

Key Takeaway
When revelation is silent on an attribute, the soundest position is to suspend judgment rather than affirm or deny based on reason alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your understanding of God where you have affirmed something without clear scriptural warrant, simply because it seemed rational or appealing? What would it cost you to suspend judgment on that matter?
?
When you encounter silence in revelation on a theological question, does that silence feel threatening—as if something is missing—or liberating, because it calls you to trust that God has revealed what is sufficient? Examine your heart: do you truly believe that revelation is enough?
?
If you were to apply the principle of waqf (suspension) not just to this attribute but to every matter where revelation is silent, what would change in how you speak about God? Would you be willing to say 'I do not know' more often, even when others demand certainty?

Objection about Ordering of Ma'nawiyya and Ma'ani
A scholar objects to the ordering of attributes and receives an answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author ordered the ma'nawiyya attributes before the ma'ani attributes, and the methodological principle behind it.

Core Idea
Ordering by agreement: start with what is undisputed before what is disputed.

This brief passage addresses a methodological objection about the ordering of attribute categories in kalam texts. The author anticipates the reader's familiarity with common practice and explains the pedagogical reasoning behind his choice. The answer, drawn from a gloss, reveals a key principle: presenting agreed-upon matters before disputed ones.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
Read the question carefully: why did the author present al-ma'nawiyya before al-ma'ani when the usual order is the opposite? Note the expectation of convention.
The objector assumes a standard practice in other books.
2
Understand the answer
The answer cites a scholar's gloss: the reason is that al-ma'nawiyya are a point of agreement, while al-ma'ani are disputed. The author finished with the agreed topic and then followed with the disputed one.
The answer does not change the content, only the order of presentation.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider the pedagogical wisdom: teaching from the certain to the less certain, from the agreed to the debated. This avoids confusion early on.
This principle applies beyond this specific case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : لِمَ قَدَّمَ المعنوية على المعاني وغالب الجاري في كتبهم العكس ؟ يجاب : بما قاله العلامة العكاري في حاشيته ( ق ١٤٣ ) : ( وجه تقديم المعنوية لأنها محل اتفاق، فلما أنهى الكلام عليها أتبعها بالمعاني )
And it may be said: Why did he present al-ma'nawiyya before al-ma'ani, while the common practice in their books is the opposite? The answer: As the scholar al-‘Akari said in his gloss (folio 143): 'The reason for presenting al-ma'nawiyya is that it is a point of agreement; so when he finished the discussion on it, he followed it with al-ma'ani.'
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector questions why the author presented the discussion of the ma'nawiyya attributes before the ma'ani attributes, contrary to the usual order in most works. The answer, drawn from al-‘Akari's gloss, explains that the ma'nawiyya attributes are agreed upon (there is no dispute about their existence), whereas the ma'ani attributes are controversial. Therefore, the author dealt with the agreed-upon topic first and then followed it with the disputed one, following a pedagogical order from the certain to the less certain.

Key Terms
المعنوية
المعاني
التقديم
الاتفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the author present al-ma'nawiyya before al-ma'ani according to al-‘Akari?
A) Because al-ma'nawiyya are more important than al-ma'ani.
B) Because al-ma'nawiyya are a point of agreement, while al-ma'ani are disputed.
C) Because al-ma'ani are derived from al-ma'nawiyya.
D) Because the author made a mistake in ordering.

Key Takeaway
The ordering of attributes in kalam texts follows pedagogical wisdom: start with what is agreed upon before addressing disagreements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

The Method of Qiyas al-Gha'ib 'ala al-Shahid and the Role of the Jami'
Sanusi explains the Mu'tazila's concession and the Sunni argument requiring a jami' in analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni theologians used the Mu'tazila's own concession to establish the analogy between witnessed and unseen, and why a unifier (jami') is necessary to avoid theological errors.

Core Idea
Analogy between the known and unknown requires a common link to avoid extremes.

In this passage, Sanusi recounts a pivotal theological exchange. The Mu'tazila admitted that in the witnessed world, attributes are distinct from essences. The Ahl al-Sunnah seized this to argue for similar distinctions in God, but the Mu'tazila countered that such analogy needs a proper unifier (jami'). This lesson unpacks that debate and clarifies key terms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila's concession
Read the opening line carefully. The Mu'tazila agreed that in the witnessed world (al-shahid), a knower knows by knowledge, a powerful is powerful by power, etc. This concession is the starting point of the argument.
2
See the Sunni argument
Notice that the Ahl al-Sunnah used this agreement to oblige the Mu'tazila to apply the same distinction to the unseen (al-gha'ib). This is an argument from consistency.
3
Grasp the Mu'tazila's objection
The Mu'tazila responded that such an analogy requires a unifier (jami'), otherwise it leads to ta'til (denying attributes) or tashbih (likening God to creation). This highlights the need for a proper methodological link.
4
Learn the definitions
Sanusi defines al-shahid as the contingent (al-hadith) or what we know, and al-gha'ib as the eternal (al-qadim) or what we do not know. These definitions ground the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أنَّ المعتزلة لمَّا ساعدَتْ على أنَّ العالِمَ القادر الحيَّ المريد في الشاهد عالم بعلم وقادر بقدرةٍ ومريد بإرادة وحي بحياة .. ألزمهم أهل السنّة رضي الله عنهم اعتبار الغائبِ بالشاهد ؛ قالوا(۳) : والجمعُ بين الغائبِ والشاهد يفتقر إلى جامعِ ، وإلا جرَّ إلى التعطيل والتشبيه . وعنَوا بالشاهد : الحادث ، وبالغائبِ : القديم ، وقيل : المراد بالشاهد : ما علمناه ، وبالغائبِ : ما لم نعلمه
Know that when the Mu'tazila agreed that the knower, powerful, living, willing in the witnessed is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, willing by will, living by life... the Ahl al-Sunnah, may Allah be pleased with them, obliged them to consider the unseen (al-gha'ib) analogously to the witnessed (al-shahid). They said (3): 'The combination between the unseen and the witnessed requires a unifier (jami'), otherwise it leads to ta'til (divesting God of attributes) and tashbih (anthropomorphism).' And they meant by al-shahid: the contingent, and by al-gha'ib: the eternal. And it is said that what is meant by al-shahid is what we know, and by al-gha'ib is what we do not know.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage sets up the foundation for qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid. The Mu'tazila had accepted that in the witnessed world, attributes are distinct from the essence. The Ahl al-Sunnah thus argued that the same should apply to God, but the Mu'tazila objected that analogy requires a common link (jami) to avoid ta'til (denial of attributes) and tashbih (likening God to creation). The passage also clarifies the terms: shahid is the contingent or what is known, gha'ib is the eternal or unknown.

Key Terms

غائب
جامع
تعطيل
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid is mere speculation without rules.
Correction: The text shows that this analogy is a disciplined method requiring a proper unifier (jami') to avoid theological errors like ta'til and tashbih.

Checkpoint

A) To prove God's existence
B) To avoid ta'til and tashbih
C) To establish anthropomorphism
D) To negate attributes

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid is a disciplined analogy requiring a proper unifier (jami') to avoid denying or likening God's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

In your own faith, where have you accepted a truth about Allah but hesitated to apply its full implications because it felt uncomfortable? How does the requirement of a jami' challenge you to be consistent in your beliefs about Allah's attributes?

The Four Unifying Principles in Theological Analogy
Sanusi enumerates and explains the four types of jami' in analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of jami' used in analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The four jami' are the logical tools that link our world to the divine.

Sanusi presents the four unifying principles (jami') that theologians use to draw analogies from the witnessed world to the unseen reality of Allah. Each type offers a different logical route to establish attributes. This passage is foundational for understanding how theological arguments are constructed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation to get an overview of the four types.
2
Analyze each jami'
For each type, note the definition, the example given, and the school that uses it.
3
Compare the methods
Consider how the jami' differ in their approach and why different schools prefer different ones.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can identify the jami' used by those who affirm states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلب : في الجوامع الأربعةِ في قياس الغائب على الشاهد ] قالوا : والجوامع أربعة (١) : جمع بالحقيقة : كقولهم : العالِمُ شاهداً : مَنْ لهُ العلم ، أو ذو العلم ، والبارى عالم ، فله علم ، وهذهِ عمده مَنْ ينفي الأحوال والجمع بالدليل : كقولهم : الإحكام شاهداً : دليل في العقل على أنَّ لفاعلِهِ علماً بهِ ، والبارى تعالى مُحكِمٌ مُتقِنُ لأفعالِهِ ، فدل على أنَّ له علماً والجمع بالشرط : كقولهم: البارى تعالى مريد ، وكل مريد قاصد لفعله ، والقصد مشروط بالعلم ، فالبارى تعالى له علم ، وإلا لثبتَ المشروط بدونِ الشرط . والجمعُ بالعلَّة - وهو عمدةُ مَنْ يثبتُ الأحوال - : كقولهم : العلم والعالمية متلازمانِ ، والعالميَّةُ مرتبةٌ على العلم ، وقد ساعدتُم على إثباتِ العالميَّةِ غائباً ، فيلزمُ مِنْ إثبات العالمية العلم ؛ فإنَّ التلازم ثابتُ بينَهما مِنَ الجانبين ، فلو صح وجود عالميَّةٍ ولا علم .. لصح ثبوت علم ولا عالمية ، ولا يقولونَ بهِ
Topic: On the four unifying principles in analogical reasoning from the unseen to the witnessed. They said: 'The unifying principles are four (1): (1) Unification by reality (jami' bi al-haqiqa): such as their saying: 'The knower in the witnessed is the one who has knowledge, or the possessor of knowledge; and the Creator is knowing, so He has knowledge.' And this is the basis of those who deny states (ahwal). (2) Unification by indication (jami' bi al-dalil): such as their saying: 'Precision (ihkam) in the witnessed is a rational indication that its agent has knowledge of it; and the Creator, exalted, is precise and perfect in His actions, so that indicates that He has knowledge.' (3) Unification by condition (jami' bi al-shart): such as their saying: 'The Creator, exalted, is willing (murid); and every willing person intends (qasid) his action; and intention is conditional on knowledge; so the Creator, exalted, has knowledge; otherwise the conditioned would exist without its condition.' (4) Unification by cause (jami' bi al-'illa) – and this is the basis of those who affirm states (ahwal): such as their saying: 'Knowledge and being-knowing (al-‘ilmiyya) are mutually entailing; and being-knowing is consequent upon knowledge; and you have agreed to establish being-knowing in the unseen; so from establishing being-knowing, knowledge necessarily follows; for the entailment is fixed between them from both sides. So if it were valid that being-knowing exists without knowledge, then it would be valid that knowledge exists without being-knowing, and they do not say that.'
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage classifies the four types of jami' used in qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid. They are: (1) jami' by reality (haqiqa), which directly posits the attribute based on the nature of the term; this is used by those who deny states. (2) jami' by indication (dalil), which uses an external sign to infer knowledge. (3) jami' by condition (shart), which uses a conditional relationship to prove knowledge. (4) jami' by cause ('illa), which relies on the entailment between attribute and its meaning; this is the method of those who affirm states. Each is illustrated with an example.

Key Terms
جامع
الحقيقة
الدليل
الشرط
العلة
الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all four jami' are equivalent and used interchangeably.
Correction: The passage shows that different theological schools prefer different jami': those who deny states use haqiqa, those who affirm states use 'illa, etc.

Checkpoint
Which type of jami' is described as the basis of those who affirm states (ahwal)?
A) Jami' bi al-haqiqa
B) Jami' bi al-dalil
C) Jami' bi al-shart
D) Jami' bi al-'illa

Key Takeaway
The four jami' provide distinct logical methods to argue from the witnessed to the unseen, each with different theological commitments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Linking by Essence (al-Jam' bil-Ḥaqīqa)
Sanusi defines the first type of link with an example about knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first type of link used in analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the absent, namely linking by essence, and how it is employed to argue for God's knowledge.

Core Idea
Linking by essence argues from a shared essential attribute in the witnessed to the same attribute in the absent.

Sanusi begins the enumeration of the four types of jawāmi' in qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid. The first type, linking by essence, asserts a shared essential predicate between the created and the Creator. An example is presented concerning knowledge: just as a knowledgeable human possesses the attribute of knowledge, so God, being knowing, must also possess knowledge. This approach is favored by those who deny the ontological status of states (aḥwāl).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the structure of the analogy sink in.
Notice how the example starts with a definition of the witnessed.
2
Identify the essence
Pinpoint the shared attribute (knowledge) that serves as the link (jāmi') between the witnessed and the absent.
The jāmi' is not the ruling itself.
3
Understand the theological implication
The passage notes that this method is used by those who deny states. Reflect on why a direct essential link avoids the need for intermediate states.
This connects to debates about the ontological status of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلب : في الجوامع الأربعةِ في قياس الغائب على الشاهدِ ] قالوا : والجوامع أربعة (١) : جمع بالحقيقة : كقولهم : العالِمُ شاهداً : مَنْ لهُ العلم ، أو ذو العلم ، والبارى عالم ، فله علم ، وهذهِ عمده مَنْ ينفي الأحوال والجمع بالدليل : كقولهم : الإحكام شاهداً : دليل في العقل على أنَّ لفاعلِهِ علماً بهِ ، والبارى تعالى مُحكِمٌ مُتقِنُ لأفعالِهِ ، فدل على أنَّ له علماً والجمع بالشرط : كقولهم: البارى تعالى مريد ، وكل مريد قاصد لفعله ، والقصد مشروط بالعلم ، فالبارى تعالى له علم ، وإلا لثبتَ المشروط بدونِ الشرط . والجمعُ بالعلَّة - وهو عمدةُ مَنْ يثبتُ الأحوال - : كقولهم : العلم والعالمية متلازمانِ ، والعالميَّةُ مرتبةٌ على العلم ، وقد ساعدتُم على إثباتِ العالميَّةِ غائباً ، فيلزمُ مِنْ إثبات العالمية العلم ؛ فإنَّ التلازم ثابتُ بينَهما مِنَ الجانبين ، فلو صح وجود عالميَّةٍ ولا علم .. لصح ثبوت علم ولا عالمية ، ولا يقولونَ بهِ (1) الجوامع : جمع جامع ؛ وهو الأمر الذي يكون سبباً في انسحاب حكم المقيس عليه على المقيس ، فالجامع غير الحكم . حامدي » ( ص ۲۲۱ ) ، وانظر » تحرير هذه القسمة في ) شرح الإرشاد ) للعلامة المقترح ( ص ١٥٩ ) . ٣١٤
Topic: On the four universals (jawāmi') in the analogical reasoning from the absent to the witnessed. They said: The universals are four: (1) Linking by essence (al-jam' bil-ḥaqīqa): as they say: The knower in the witnessed world is one who has knowledge, or possesses knowledge, and the Creator is knowing, so He has knowledge. This is the mainstay of those who deny states (aḥwāl). (2) Linking by evidence (al-jam' bil-dalīl): as they say: The perfection of craftsmanship in the witnessed world is an intellectual evidence that its agent has knowledge of it, and the Creator, exalted, is perfect in His actions, so it indicates that He has knowledge. (3) Linking by condition (al-jam' bil-sharṭ): as they say: The Creator, exalted, is willing, and every willing one intends his action, and intention is conditioned by knowledge, so the Creator, exalted, has knowledge; otherwise the conditioned would exist without the condition. (4) Linking by cause (al-jam' bil-'illa) – and this is the mainstay of those who affirm states: as they say: Knowledge and knowership (al-'ālimiyya) are mutually entailing, and knowership is dependent on knowledge, and you have agreed to affirm knowership in the absent, so from the affirmation of knowership follows knowledge; for the mutual entailment is established between them from both sides. If a knowership without knowledge were valid, then a knowledge without knowership would be valid, and they do not say that. (1) al-jawāmi': plural of jāmi'; it is the thing that is the reason for the extension of the ruling of the analogized-from to the analogized-to; so the jāmi' is not the ruling. Ḥāmidī (p. 221), and see the analysis of this division in Sharḥ al-Irshād by al-Muqtraḥ (p. 159).
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a methodological classification. The author presents the four types of 'jāmi'' (link or common factor) used in qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid, i.e., reasoning from the observed world (the witnessed) to the unseen divine realm (the absent). Each type is defined with an example related to the divine attribute of knowledge. The first type is linking by essence (al-jam' bil-ḥaqīqa): it asserts a common essential attribute, e.g., both the human knower and God are described as 'knowing', so God must have knowledge. This is the method of those who deny the ontological reality of states (aḥwāl). The second type is linking by evidence (al-jam' bil-dalīl): the perfection of craftsmanship in the observed world indicates that its agent has knowledge; God's perfect creation similarly indicates knowledge. The third type is linking by condition (al-jam' bil-sharṭ): every willing agent intends his action, and intention requires knowledge; since God is willing, He must have knowledge, otherwise a conditioned thing would exist without its condition, which is impossible. The fourth type is linking by cause (al-jam' bil-'illa): it posits mutual entailment between two attributes, here knowledge and 'knowership' (al-'ālimiyya). Since the opponent agrees that God has knowership (the attribute of being knowing), and knowership necessarily entails knowledge due to their mutual entailment, God must have knowledge. This is the method of those who affirm states (aḥwāl). The footnote explains the term 'jāmi'' and provides references.

Key Terms
الجمع بالحقيقة (al-jam' bil-ḥaqīqa)
الجوامع (al-jawāmi')
الغائب (al-ghā'ib)
الشاهد (al-shāhid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that linking by essence means the link is the ruling itself.
Correction: The jāmi' is not the ruling; it is the basis for extending the ruling. Here, the common essence 'knowledge' is the link, not the ruling of 'knowing'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group primarily uses linking by essence?
A) Those who affirm states
B) Those who deny states
C) The Mu'tazila
D) The Ash'aris

Key Takeaway
Linking by essence directly transfers an essential attribute from the witnessed to the absent without intermediate states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson's distinction between linking by essence and intermediate states change how you think about God's attributes?
?
In your daily life, do you ever catch yourself making analogies about God based on human experience? This lesson warns that linking by essence is valid only when the attribute is essential. What does that mean for how you understand God's mercy, power, or will?
?
This lesson emphasizes that those who deny states (aḥwāl) use linking by essence. Have you ever considered that your own understanding of God's attributes might be inadvertently anthropomorphic? What step can you take today to ensure your belief aligns with the methodology of Ahl al-Sunna?

Linking by Evidence (al-Jam' bil-Dalīl)
Sanusi illustrates the second type of link using the example of craftsmanship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second type of link, linking by evidence, where the perfection of craftsmanship in the witnessed indicates the agent's knowledge, and analogically God's perfect creation indicates His knowledge.

Core Idea
Linking by evidence uses observable signs as proof for divine attributes.

The second type of jāmi' is linking by evidence. Here, the common factor is not an essential attribute but an external indication: the perfection of an act points to the knowledge of its agent. Sanusi gives the example of craftsmanship: in the witnessed, a perfectly crafted object demonstrates that its maker had knowledge; similarly, God's perfect creation demonstrates that He has knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the argument moves from an observed effect to an unseen cause.
This is a type of causal inference.
2
Distinguish from essence
Compare with the first type. Here the link is not a shared attribute but a shared evidence pattern.
The evidence is 'perfection of craftsmanship'.
3
Reflect on its use
The passage does not associate this type with a specific theological group. Consider why it might be widely acceptable.
It relies on empirical reasoning.

Topic: On the four universals (jawāmi') in the analogical reasoning from the absent to the witnessed. They said: The universals are four: (1) Linking by essence (al-jam' bil-ḥaqīqa): as they say: The knower in the witnessed world is one who has knowledge, or possesses knowledge, and the Creator is knowing, so He has knowledge. This is the mainstay of those who deny states (aḥwāl). (2) Linking by evidence (al-jam' bil-dalīl): as they say: The perfection of craftsmanship in the witnessed world is an intellectual evidence that its agent has knowledge of it, and the Creator, exalted, is perfect in His actions, so it indicates that He has knowledge. (3) Linking by condition (al-jam' bil-sharṭ): as they say: The Creator, exalted, is willing, and every willing one intends his action, and intention is conditioned by knowledge, so the Creator, exalted, has knowledge; otherwise the conditioned would exist without the condition. (4) Linking by cause (al-jam' bil-'illa) – and this is the mainstay of those who affirm states: as they say: Knowledge and knowership (al-'ālimiyya) are mutually entailing, and knowership is dependent on knowledge, and you have agreed to affirm knowership in the absent, so from the affirmation of knowership follows knowledge; for the mutual entailment is established between them from both sides. If a knowership without knowledge were valid, then a knowledge without knowership would be valid, and they do not say that. (1) al-jawāmi': plural of jāmi'; it is the thing that is the reason for the extension of the ruling of the analogized-from to the analogized-to; so the jāmi' is not the ruling. Ḥāmidī (p. 221), and see the analysis of this division in Sharḥ al-Irshād by al-Muqtraḥ (p. 159).
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a methodological classification. The author presents the four types of 'jāmi'' (link or common factor) used in qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid, i.e., reasoning from the observed world (the witnessed) to the unseen divine realm (the absent). Each type is defined with an example related to the divine attribute of knowledge. The first type is linking by essence (al-jam' bil-ḥaqīqa): it asserts a common essential attribute, e.g., both the human knower and God are described as 'knowing', so God must have knowledge. This is the method of those who deny the ontological reality of states (aḥwāl). The second type is linking by evidence (al-jam' bil-dalīl): the perfection of craftsmanship in the observed world indicates that its agent has knowledge; God's perfect creation similarly indicates knowledge. The third type is linking by condition (al-jam' bil-sharṭ): every willing agent intends his action, and intention requires knowledge; since God is willing, He must have knowledge, otherwise a conditioned thing would exist without its condition, which is impossible. The fourth type is linking by cause (al-jam' bil-'illa): it posits mutual entailment between two attributes, here knowledge and 'knowership' (al-'ālimiyya). Since the opponent agrees that God has knowership (the attribute of being knowing), and knowership necessarily entails knowledge due to their mutual entailment, God must have knowledge. This is the method of those who affirm states (aḥwāl). The footnote explains the term 'jāmi'' and provides references.

Key Terms
الجمع بالدليل (al-jam' bil-dalīl)
الإحكام (al-iḥkām)
الدليل (al-dalīl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In linking by evidence, what serves as the evidence for God's knowledge?
A) The fact that God is called 'knowing'
B) The perfection of His creation
C) The existence of human knowledge
D) The necessity of intention

Key Takeaway
Linking by evidence infers a divine attribute from observable signs in creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe a flaw in your life or in nature, do you still see the underlying perfection that points to Allah's knowledge, or does that imperfection cause you to doubt His awareness of your affairs? How can you train your eyes to see the 'الإحكام' even in difficulty?
?
If the perfection of a single leaf proves that its Creator knows its every detail, then what does your anxiety about the future say about your trust in that knowledge? Will you commit today to pause before every worry and say, 'He knows, and that is enough'?
?
This lesson shows that perfect design is a direct proof of knowledge. Have you ever used the order in your own body—your heartbeat, your thoughts—as a personal testimony to Allah's knowledge? What would change if you consciously recited this proof every night before sleep?
